<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Childhood_.281685.E2.80.931703.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_(1685–1703)">Childhood (1685–1703)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg/170px-Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg/255px-Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Johann_Ambrosius_Bach" title="Johann Ambrosius Bach">Johann Ambrosius Bach</a>, 1685, Bach's father. Painting attributed to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Johann_David_Herlicius&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Johann David Herlicius (page does not exist)">Johann David Herlicius</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_David_Herlicius" title="de:Johann David Herlicius">de</a>]</span></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bach_family" title="Bach family">Bach family</a></div>
<p>Johann Sebastian Bach<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPA_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPA-3">[n 2]</a></sup> was born in <a href="/wiki/Eisenach" title="Eisenach">Eisenach</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Saxe-Eisenach" title="Saxe-Eisenach">duchy of Saxe-Eisenach</a>, in present-day Germany, on 21 March 1685 <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> (31 March 1685 <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">N.S.</a>). He was the eighth and youngest child of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ambrosius_Bach" title="Johann Ambrosius Bach">Johann Ambrosius Bach</a>, the director of the town musicians, and <a href="/wiki/Maria_Elisabeth_L%C3%A4mmerhirt" title="Maria Elisabeth Lämmerhirt">Maria Elisabeth Lämmerhirt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones20073_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones20073-9">[7]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeck2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidN1zSVDYTCXgCpgPA2_2],_[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidN1zSVDYTCXgCpgPA156_156]_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeck2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidN1zSVDYTCXgCpgPA2_2],_[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidN1zSVDYTCXgCpgPA156_156]-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyd20006_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyd20006-11">[9]</a></sup>  His father likely taught him violin and basic music theory. His uncles were all professional musicians, whose posts included church organists, court chamber musicians, and composers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolffEmeryWollnyLeisinger2018II._List_of_all_family_members_alphabetically_by_first_name_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolffEmeryWollnyLeisinger2018II._List_of_all_family_members_alphabetically_by_first_name-12">[10]</a></sup> One uncle, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Bach_(musician_at_Arnstadt)" title="Johann Christoph Bach (musician at Arnstadt)">Johann Christoph Bach</a> (1645–1693), introduced him to the organ,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolffEmery2001_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolffEmery2001-13">[11]</a></sup> and an older second cousin, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ludwig_Bach" title="Johann Ludwig Bach">Johann Ludwig Bach</a> (1677–1731), was a well-known composer and violinist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolffEmeryWollnyLeisinger2018II._List_of_all_family_members_alphabetically_by_first_name_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolffEmeryWollnyLeisinger2018II._List_of_all_family_members_alphabetically_by_first_name-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[n 3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bach's mother died in 1694, and his father died eight months later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles196286–87_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles196286–87-15">[12]</a></sup> The 10-year-old Bach moved in with his eldest brother, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Bach_(organist_at_Ohrdruf)" title="Johann Christoph Bach (organist at Ohrdruf)">Johann Christoph Bach</a> (1671–1721), the organist at <a href="/wiki/Michaeliskirche_(Ohrdruf)" title="Michaeliskirche (Ohrdruf)">St. Michael's Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ohrdruf" title="Ohrdruf">Ohrdruf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxe-Gotha-Altenburg" title="Saxe-Gotha-Altenburg">Saxe-Gotha-Altenburg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyd20007–8_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyd20007–8-16">[13]</a></sup> There he studied, performed, and copied music, including his own brother's, despite being forbidden to do so because scores were so valuable and private, and blank ledger paper of that type was costly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidMendelWolff1998299_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidMendelWolff1998299-17">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff200045_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff200045-18">[15]</a></sup> He received valuable teaching from his brother, who instructed him on the <a href="/wiki/Clavichord" title="Clavichord">clavichord</a>. Johann Christoph exposed him to the works of great composers of the day, including South Germans such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Caspar_Kerll" title="Johann Caspar Kerll">Johann Caspar Kerll</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Jakob_Froberger" title="Johann Jakob Froberger">Johann Jakob Froberger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Pachelbel" title="Johann Pachelbel">Johann Pachelbel</a> (under whom Johann Christoph had studied); North Germans;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff200019,_46_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff200019,_46-19">[16]</a></sup> Frenchmen such as <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lully" title="Jean-Baptiste Lully">Jean-Baptiste Lully</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Marchand" title="Louis Marchand">Louis Marchand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marin_Marais" title="Marin Marais">Marin Marais</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff200073_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff200073-20">[17]</a></sup> and even the Italian <a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Frescobaldi" title="Girolamo Frescobaldi">Girolamo Frescobaldi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff2000170_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff2000170-21">[18]</a></sup> During this time, he was also taught theology, Latin and Greek at the local <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">gymnasium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitta1899a186–187_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitta1899a186–187-22">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 3 April 1700, Bach and his schoolfriend Georg Erdmann – who was two years Bach's elder – were enrolled in the prestigious St. Michael's School in <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCneburg" title="Lüneburg">Lüneburg</a>, some two weeks' travel north of Ohrdruf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff200041–43_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff200041–43-23">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EidamChI_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-EidamChI-24">[21]</a></sup> Their journey was probably undertaken mostly on foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EidamChI_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-EidamChI-24">[21]</a></sup> His two years there were critical in exposing Bach to a wider range of European culture. In addition to singing in the choir, he played the school's three-manual organ and harpsichords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-25">[22]</a></sup> He also came into contact with sons of aristocrats from northern Germany who had been sent to the nearby <i>Ritter-Academie</i> to prepare for careers in other disciplines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff200055–56_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff200055–56-26">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Weimar.2C_Arnstadt.2C_and_M.C3.BChlhausen_.281703.E2.80.931708.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Weimar,_Arnstadt,_and_Mühlhausen_(1703–1708)">Weimar, Arnstadt, and Mühlhausen (1703–1708)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Wender-Orgel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="736" data-file-width="774" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Wender-Orgel.jpg/220px-Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Wender-Orgel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Wender-Orgel.jpg/330px-Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Wender-Orgel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Wender-Orgel.jpg/440px-Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Wender-Orgel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Wender-Orgel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Wender" title="Johann Friedrich Wender">Wender</a> organ Bach played in Arnstadt</div></div></div>
<p>In January 1703, shortly after graduating from St. Michael's and being turned down for the post of organist at <a href="/wiki/Sangerhausen" title="Sangerhausen">Sangerhausen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERich199527_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERich199527-27">[24]</a></sup> Bach was appointed court musician in the chapel of Duke <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ernst_III,_Duke_of_Saxe-Weimar" title="Johann Ernst III, Duke of Saxe-Weimar">Johann Ernst III</a> in <a href="/wiki/Weimar" title="Weimar">Weimar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyd200015–16_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyd200015–16-28">[25]</a></sup> His role there is unclear, but it probably included menial, non-musical duties. During his seven-month tenure at Weimar, his reputation as a keyboardist spread so much that he was invited to inspect the new organ and give the inaugural recital at the New Church (now <a href="/wiki/Bach_Church,_Arnstadt" title="Bach Church, Arnstadt">Bach Church</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Arnstadt" title="Arnstadt">Arnstadt</a>, located about 30 kilometres (19 mi) southwest of Weimar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChiapusso196862_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChiapusso196862-29">[26]</a></sup> On 14 August 1703, he became the organist at the New Church,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolffEmery2001_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolffEmery2001-13">[11]</a></sup> with light duties, a relatively generous salary, and a new organ tuned in a temperament that allowed music written in a wider range of keys to be played.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams2003a40_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams2003a40-30">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite strong family connections and a musically enthusiastic employer, tension built up between Bach and the authorities after several years in the post. Bach was dissatisfied with the standard of singers in the choir. He called one of them a "Zippel Fagottist" (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/weenie" title="wiktionary:weenie">weenie</a> bassoon player). Late one evening this student, named Geyersbach, went after Bach with a stick. Bach filed a complaint against Geyersbach with the authorities. They acquitted Geyersbach with a minor reprimand and ordered Bach to be more moderate regarding the musical qualities he expected from his students. Some months later Bach upset his employer by a prolonged absence from Arnstadt: after obtaining leave for four weeks, he was absent for around four months in 1705–1706 to visit the organist and composer <a href="/wiki/Dieterich_Buxtehude" title="Dieterich Buxtehude">Dieterich Buxtehude</a> in the northern city of <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCbeck" title="Lübeck">Lübeck</a>. The visit to Buxtehude involved a 450-kilometre (280 mi) journey each way, reportedly on foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolff2000pp83ff_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff2000pp83ff-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1706, Bach applied for a post as organist at the <a href="/wiki/Divi_Blasii" title="Divi Blasii">Blasius Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BChlhausen" title="Mühlhausen">Mühlhausen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WolffP102_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-WolffP102-33">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams2003a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidwX-rF2_k_S8CpgPA38_38–39]_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams2003a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidwX-rF2_k_S8CpgPA38_38–39]-34">[31]</a></sup> As part of his application, he had a <a href="/wiki/Cantata" title="Cantata">cantata</a> performed on Easter, 24 April 1707, likely an early version of his <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Christ_lag_in_Todes_Banden,_BWV_4" title="Christ lag in Todes Banden, BWV 4">Christ lag in Todes Banden</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup> A month later Bach's application was accepted and he took up the post in July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WolffP102_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-WolffP102-33">[30]</a></sup> The position included significantly higher remuneration, improved conditions, and a better choir. Four months after arriving at Mühlhausen, Bach married <a href="/wiki/Maria_Barbara_Bach" title="Maria Barbara Bach">Maria Barbara Bach</a>, his second cousin. Bach was able to convince the church and town government at Mühlhausen to fund an expensive renovation of the organ at the Blasius Church. In 1708 Bach wrote <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Gott_ist_mein_K%C3%B6nig,_BWV_71" title="Gott ist mein König, BWV 71">Gott ist mein König</a></i></span>, a festive <a href="/wiki/Church_cantata_(Bach)#New_council" title="Church cantata (Bach)">cantata for the inauguration of the new council</a>, which was published at the council's expense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-25">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Return_to_Weimar_.281708.E2.80.931717.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_Weimar_(1708–1717)">Return to Weimar (1708–1717)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leipzig_Paulinerkirche_Scheibe-Orgel_um_1720.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4207" data-file-width="2154" decoding="async" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Leipzig_Paulinerkirche_Scheibe-Orgel_um_1720.jpg/170px-Leipzig_Paulinerkirche_Scheibe-Orgel_um_1720.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Leipzig_Paulinerkirche_Scheibe-Orgel_um_1720.jpg/255px-Leipzig_Paulinerkirche_Scheibe-Orgel_um_1720.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Leipzig_Paulinerkirche_Scheibe-Orgel_um_1720.jpg/340px-Leipzig_Paulinerkirche_Scheibe-Orgel_um_1720.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leipzig_Paulinerkirche_Scheibe-Orgel_um_1720.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Organ of the <a href="/wiki/Paulinerkirche,_Leipzig" title="Paulinerkirche, Leipzig">St. Paul's Church in Leipzig</a>, tested by Bach in 1717.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Erschallet,_ihr_Lieder,_erklinget,_ihr_Saiten!_BWV_172#Background" title="Erschallet, ihr Lieder, erklinget, ihr Saiten! BWV 172">Erschallet, ihr Lieder, erklinget, ihr Saiten! BWV 172 § Background</a></div>
<p>Bach left Mühlhausen in 1708, returning to Weimar this time as organist and from 1714 <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Konzertmeister</i></span> (director of music) at the ducal court, where he had an opportunity to work with a large, well-funded contingent of professional musicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-25">[22]</a></sup> Bach and his wife moved into a house close to the ducal palace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> Later the same year, their first child, Catharina Dorothea, was born, and Maria Barbara's elder, unmarried sister joined them. She remained to help run the household until her death in 1729. Three sons were also born in Weimar: <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Friedemann_Bach" title="Wilhelm Friedemann Bach">Wilhelm Friedemann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Philipp_Emanuel_Bach" title="Carl Philipp Emanuel Bach">Carl Philipp Emanuel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Bernhard_Bach" title="Johann Gottfried Bernhard Bach">Johann Gottfried Bernhard</a>. Johann Sebastian and Maria Barbara had three more children, who however did not live to their first birthday, including twins born in 1713.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForkel1920[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastian01terrgoogpagen358mode1up_Table_VII,_p._309]_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForkel1920[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastian01terrgoogpagen358mode1up_Table_VII,_p._309]-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bach's time in Weimar was the start of a sustained period of composing keyboard and orchestral works. He attained the proficiency and confidence to extend the prevailing structures and include influences from abroad. He learned to write dramatic openings and employ the dynamic rhythms and harmonic schemes found in the music of Italians such as <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Vivaldi" title="Antonio Vivaldi">Vivaldi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arcangelo_Corelli" title="Arcangelo Corelli">Corelli</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Torelli" title="Giuseppe Torelli">Torelli</a>. Bach absorbed these stylistic aspects in part by transcribing Vivaldi's string and wind <a href="/wiki/Concerto" title="Concerto">concertos</a> for harpsichord and organ; many of these transcribed works are still regularly performed. Bach was particularly attracted to the Italian style, in which one or more solo instruments alternate section-by-section with the full orchestra throughout a <a href="/wiki/Movement_(music)" title="Movement (music)">movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Weimar, Bach continued to play and compose for the organ and perform concert music with the duke's ensemble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-25">[22]</a></sup> He also began to write the <a href="/wiki/Prelude_(music)" title="Prelude (music)">preludes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fugue" title="Fugue">fugues</a> which were later assembled into his monumental work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Well-Tempered_Clavier" title="The Well-Tempered Clavier">The Well-Tempered Clavier</a></i> ("clavier" meaning clavichord or harpsichord),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChiapusso1968168_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChiapusso1968168-39">[36]</a></sup> consisting of two books,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchweitzer1923331_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchweitzer1923331-40">[37]</a></sup> each containing 24 preludes and fugues in every <a href="/wiki/Major_scale" title="Major scale">major</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minor_scale" title="Minor scale">minor</a> key. Bach also started work on the <i><a href="/wiki/Orgelb%C3%BCchlein" title="Orgelbüchlein">Little Organ Book</a></i> in Weimar, containing traditional <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_chorale" title="Lutheran chorale">Lutheran chorale</a> tunes set in complex textures. In 1713, Bach was offered a post in <a href="/wiki/Halle_(Saale)" title="Halle (Saale)">Halle</a> when he advised the authorities during a renovation by Christoph Cuntzius of the main organ in the west gallery of the <a href="/wiki/Marktkirche_Unser_Lieben_Frauen" title="Marktkirche Unser Lieben Frauen">Market Church of Our Dear Lady</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sadie1998p205_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sadie1998p205-42">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the spring of 1714, Bach was promoted to <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Konzertmeister</i></span>, an honour that entailed performing a church cantata monthly in the castle church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff2000147,_156_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff2000147,_156-43">[40]</a></sup> The first three cantatas in the new series Bach composed in Weimar were <span title="German-language text"><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Himmelsk%C3%B6nig,_sei_willkommen,_BWV_182" title="Himmelskönig, sei willkommen, BWV 182"><i>Himmelskönig, sei willkommen</i>, BWV 182</a></span></span>, for <a href="/wiki/Palm_Sunday" title="Palm Sunday">Palm Sunday</a>, which coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Annunciation" title="Annunciation">Annunciation</a> that year; <span title="German-language text"><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Weinen,_Klagen,_Sorgen,_Zagen,_BWV_12" title="Weinen, Klagen, Sorgen, Zagen, BWV 12"><i>Weinen, Klagen, Sorgen, Zagen</i>, BWV 12</a></span></span>, for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jubilate_Sunday" title="Jubilate Sunday">Jubilate Sunday</a>; and <span title="German-language text"><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Erschallet,_ihr_Lieder,_erklinget,_ihr_Saiten!_BWV_172" title="Erschallet, ihr Lieder, erklinget, ihr Saiten! BWV 172"><i>Erschallet, ihr Lieder, erklinget, ihr Saiten!</i>  BWV 172</a></span></span> for <a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Pentecost</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolff30_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff30-44">[41]</a></sup> Bach's first Christmas cantata, <span title="German-language text"><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Christen,_%C3%A4tzet_diesen_Tag,_BWV_63" title="Christen, ätzet diesen Tag, BWV 63"><i>Christen, ätzet diesen Tag</i>, BWV 63</a></span></span>, was premiered in 1714 or 1715.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1717, Bach eventually fell out of favour in Weimar and, according to a translation of the court secretary's report, was jailed for almost a month before being unfavourably dismissed: "On November 6, [1717], the quondam [former] concertmaster and organist Bach was confined to the County Judge's place of detention for too stubbornly forcing the issue of his dismissal and finally on December 2 was freed from arrest with notice of his unfavourable discharge."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidMendelWolff199880_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidMendelWolff199880-47">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="K.C3.B6then_.281717.E2.80.931723.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Köthen_(1717–1723)">Köthen (1717–1723)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BWV1001-cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1460" data-file-width="970" decoding="async" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/BWV1001-cropped.jpg/220px-BWV1001-cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/BWV1001-cropped.jpg/330px-BWV1001-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/BWV1001-cropped.jpg/440px-BWV1001-cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BWV1001-cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bach's autograph of the first movement of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_1001" title="BWV 1001">first sonata for solo violin, BWV 1001</a><audio controls="" data-durationhint="176" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Sonata_in_in_G_m_J.S.Bach_(1_mov_prelude).ogg" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="69203" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (69 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/Sonata_in_in_G_m_J.S.Bach_%281_mov_prelude%29.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="154208" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/ee/Sonata_in_in_G_m_J.S.Bach_%281_mov_prelude%29.ogg/Sonata_in_in_G_m_J.S.Bach_%281_mov_prelude%29.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Leopold,_Prince_of_Anhalt-K%C3%B6then" title="Leopold, Prince of Anhalt-Köthen">Leopold, Prince of Anhalt-Köthen</a>, hired Bach to serve as his <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Kapellmeister" title="Kapellmeister">Kapellmeister</a></i></span> (director of music) in 1717. Prince Leopold, himself a musician, appreciated Bach's talents, paid him well and gave him considerable latitude in composing and performing. The prince was a <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a> and did not use elaborate music in his worship; accordingly, most of Bach's work from this period was secular,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles196257_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles196257-48">[45]</a></sup> including the <a href="/wiki/Orchestral_suites_(Bach)" title="Orchestral suites (Bach)">orchestral suites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cello_Suites_(Bach)" title="Cello Suites (Bach)">cello suites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sonatas_and_Partitas_for_Solo_Violin_(Bach)" title="Sonatas and Partitas for Solo Violin (Bach)">sonatas and partitas for solo violin</a>, and <i>Brandenburg Concertos</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyd200074_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyd200074-49">[46]</a></sup> Bach also composed secular cantatas for the court, such as <span title="German-language text"><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Die_Zeit,_die_Tag_und_Jahre_macht,_BWV_134a" title="Die Zeit, die Tag und Jahre macht, BWV 134a"><i>Die Zeit, die Tag und Jahre macht</i>, BWV 134a</a></span></span>.
</p><p>Despite being born in the same year and only about 130 kilometres (80 mi) apart, Bach and <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">Handel</a> never met. In 1719, Bach made the 35-kilometre (22 mi) journey from <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6then_(Anhalt)" title="Köthen (Anhalt)">Köthen</a> to <a href="/wiki/Halle_(Saale)" title="Halle (Saale)">Halle</a> with the intention of meeting Handel; however, Handel had left the town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Til200769,_372_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Til200769,_372-50">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDent200423_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDent200423-51">[48]</a></sup> In 1730, Bach's oldest son, Wilhelm Friedemann, travelled to Halle to invite Handel to visit the Bach family in Leipzig, but the visit did not take place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpaeth193737_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpaeth193737-52">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 7 July 1720, while Bach was away in <a href="/wiki/Karlovy_Vary" title="Karlovy Vary">Carlsbad</a> with Prince Leopold, Bach's wife suddenly died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b11_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b11-53">[50]</a></sup> The following year, he met <a href="/wiki/Anna_Magdalena_Bach" title="Anna Magdalena Bach">Anna Magdalena Wilcke</a>, a young, highly gifted soprano 16 years his junior, who performed at the court in Köthen; they married on 3 December 1721.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeiringer196650_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeiringer196650-54">[51]</a></sup> Together they had 13 children, six of whom survived into adulthood: <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Heinrich_Bach" title="Gottfried Heinrich Bach">Gottfried Heinrich</a>; Elisabeth Juliane Friederica (1726–1781); <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Friedrich_Bach" title="Johann Christoph Friedrich Bach">Johann Christoph Friedrich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christian_Bach" title="Johann Christian Bach">Johann Christian</a>, who both, especially Johann Christian, became significant musicians; Johanna Carolina (1737–1781); and Regina Susanna (1742–1809).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff198398,_111_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff198398,_111-55">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Leipzig_.281723.E2.80.931750.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Leipzig_(1723–1750)">Leipzig (1723–1750)</span></h3>
<p>In 1723, Bach was appointed <i><a href="/wiki/Thomaskantor" title="Thomaskantor">Thomaskantor</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Cantor_(Christianity)" title="Cantor (Christianity)">Cantor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/St._Thomas_School,_Leipzig" title="St. Thomas School, Leipzig">St. Thomas School</a> at the <a href="/wiki/St._Thomas_Church,_Leipzig" title="St. Thomas Church, Leipzig">St. Thomas Church</a> in Leipzig, which provided music for four churches in the city: the St. Thomas Church and <a href="/wiki/St._Nicholas_Church,_Leipzig" title="St. Nicholas Church, Leipzig">St. Nicholas Church</a> and to a lesser extent the <a href="/wiki/St._Matthew,_Leipzig" title="St. Matthew, Leipzig">New Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Peter,_Leipzig" title="St. Peter, Leipzig">St. Peter's Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastianb02spituoftpage192mode2up_192–193]_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastianb02spituoftpage192mode2up_192–193]-56">[53]</a></sup> This was "the leading cantorate in Protestant Germany",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolff253_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff253-57">[54]</a></sup> located in the mercantile city in the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Saxony" title="Electorate of Saxony">Electorate of Saxony</a>, which he held for 27 years until his death. During that time he gained further prestige through honorary appointments at the courts of Köthen and Weissenfels, as well as that of the Elector <a href="/wiki/Augustus_II_the_Strong" title="Augustus II the Strong">Frederick Augustus</a> (who was also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Poland" title="King of Poland">King of Poland</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolff253_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff253-57">[54]</a></sup> Bach frequently disagreed with his employer, Leipzig's city council, which he regarded as "penny-pinching".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff2013345_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff2013345-58">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Appointment_in_Leipzig">Appointment in Leipzig</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomaskirche_und_-schule_1723.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1360" data-file-width="1040" decoding="async" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Thomaskirche_und_-schule_1723.jpg/220px-Thomaskirche_und_-schule_1723.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Thomaskirche_und_-schule_1723.jpg/330px-Thomaskirche_und_-schule_1723.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Thomaskirche_und_-schule_1723.jpg/440px-Thomaskirche_und_-schule_1723.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomaskirche_und_-schule_1723.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St._Thomas_Church,_Leipzig" title="St. Thomas Church, Leipzig">St. Thomas Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Thomas_School,_Leipzig" title="St. Thomas School, Leipzig">School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a> in 1723</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johann_Kuhnau" title="Johann Kuhnau">Johann Kuhnau</a> had been Thomaskantor in Leipzig from 1701 until his death on 5 June 1722. Bach had visited Leipzig during Kuhnau's tenure: in 1714 he attended the service at the St. Thomas Church on the first Sunday of Advent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastianb02spituoftpage265mode1up_265]_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastianb02spituoftpage265mode1up_265]-59">[56]</a></sup> and in 1717 he had tested the organ of the <a href="/wiki/Paulinerkirche,_Leipzig" title="Paulinerkirche, Leipzig">St. Paul's Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastianb02spituoftpage184mode1up_184]_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastianb02spituoftpage184mode1up_184]-60">[57]</a></sup> In 1716 Bach and Kuhnau had met on the occasion of the testing and inauguration of an organ in Halle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sadie1998p205_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sadie1998p205-42">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>After being offered the position, Bach was invited to Leipzig only after <a href="/wiki/Georg_Philipp_Telemann" title="Georg Philipp Telemann">Georg Philipp Telemann</a> indicated that he would not be interested in relocating to Leipzig.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BL_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-BL-61">[58]</a></sup> Telemann went to Hamburg, where he "had his own struggles with the city's senate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff2013348_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff2013348-62">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bach was required to instruct the students of the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Thomasschule</i></span> in singing and provide church music for the main churches in Leipzig. He was also assigned to teach Latin but was allowed to employ four "prefects" (deputies) to do this instead. The prefects also aided with musical instruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff2013349_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff2013349-63">[60]</a></sup> A cantata was required for the church services on Sundays and additional church holidays during the <a href="/wiki/Church_cantata_(Bach)" title="Church cantata (Bach)">liturgical year</a>.
</p>
<h4><span id="Cantata_cycle_years_.281723.E2.80.931729.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cantata_cycle_years_(1723–1729)">Cantata cycle years (1723–1729)</span></h4>
<p>Bach usually led performances of his <a href="/wiki/Bach_cantata" title="Bach cantata">cantatas</a>, most of which were composed within three years of his relocation to Leipzig. The first was <span title="German-language text"><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Die_Elenden_sollen_essen,_BWV_75" title="Die Elenden sollen essen, BWV 75"><i>Die Elenden sollen essen</i>, BWV 75</a></span></span>, performed in the St. Nicholas Church on 30 May 1723, the first Sunday after <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Sunday" title="Trinity Sunday">Trinity</a>. Bach collected his cantatas in annual cycles. Five are mentioned in obituaries, three are extant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolff30_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff30-44">[41]</a></sup> Of the more than 300 cantatas which Bach composed in Leipzig, over 100 have been lost to posterity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolff5_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff5-64">[61]</a></sup> Most of these works expound on the Gospel readings prescribed for every Sunday and feast day in the Lutheran year. Bach started a second annual cycle the first Sunday after Trinity of 1724 and composed only <a href="/wiki/Chorale_cantata_(Bach)" title="Chorale cantata (Bach)">chorale cantatas</a>, each based on a single church hymn. These include <span title="German-language text"><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/O_Ewigkeit,_du_Donnerwort,_BWV_20" title="O Ewigkeit, du Donnerwort, BWV 20"><i>O Ewigkeit, du Donnerwort</i>, BWV 20</a></span></span>, <span title="German-language text"><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Wachet_auf,_ruft_uns_die_Stimme,_BWV_140" title="Wachet auf, ruft uns die Stimme, BWV 140"><i>Wachet auf, ruft uns die Stimme</i>, BWV 140</a></span></span>, <span title="German-language text"><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Nun_komm,_der_Heiden_Heiland,_BWV_62" title="Nun komm, der Heiden Heiland, BWV 62"><i>Nun komm, der Heiden Heiland</i>, BWV 62</a></span></span>, and <span title="German-language text"><span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Wie_sch%C3%B6n_leuchtet_der_Morgenstern,_BWV_1" title="Wie schön leuchtet der Morgenstern, BWV 1"><i>Wie schön leuchtet der Morgenstern</i>, BWV 1</a></span></span>.
</p><p>Bach drew the soprano and alto choristers from the school and the tenors and basses from the school and elsewhere in Leipzig. Performing at weddings and funerals provided extra income for these groups; it was probably for this purpose, and for in-school training, that he wrote at least six <a href="/wiki/Motet" title="Motet">motets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup> As part of his regular church work, he performed other composers' motets, which served as formal models for his own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bach's predecessor as cantor, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Kuhnau" title="Johann Kuhnau">Johann Kuhnau</a>, had also been music director for the St. Paul's Church, the church of <a href="/wiki/Leipzig_University" title="Leipzig University">Leipzig University</a>. But when Bach was installed as cantor in 1723, he was put in charge only of music for festal (church holiday) services at the St. Paul's Church; his petition to also provide music for regular Sunday services there (for a corresponding salary increase) went all the way to the Elector but was denied. After this, in 1725, Bach "lost interest" in working even for festal services at the St. Paul's Church and appeared there only on "special occasions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyd2000112–113_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyd2000112–113-67">[64]</a></sup> The St. Paul's Church had a much better and newer (1716) organ than did the St. Thomas Church or the St. Nicholas Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b288–290_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b288–290-68">[65]</a></sup> Bach was not required to play any organ in his official duties, but it is believed he liked to play on the St. Paul's Church organ "for his own pleasure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b281,_287_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitta1899b281,_287-69">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1440" data-file-width="709" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg/130px-Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg/195px-Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg/260px-Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_Zimmermann" title="Café Zimmermann">Café Zimmermann</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1720</span></div></div></div>
<p>Bach broadened his composing and performing beyond the liturgy by taking over, in March 1729, the directorship of the <a href="/wiki/Collegium_Musicum" title="Collegium Musicum">Collegium Musicum</a>, a secular performance ensemble started by Telemann. This was one of the dozens of private societies in the major German-speaking cities that were established by musically active university students; these societies had become increasingly important in public musical life and were typically led by the most prominent professionals in a city. In the words of <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Wolff" title="Christoph Wolff">Christoph Wolff</a>, assuming the directorship was a shrewd move that "consolidated Bach's firm grip on Leipzig's principal musical institutions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff2000341_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff2000341-70">[67]</a></sup> Every week the <i>Collegium Musicum</i> would give two-hour performances in winter at the  <a href="/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_Zimmermann" title="Café Zimmermann">Café Zimmermann</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Coffeehouse" title="Coffeehouse">coffeehouse</a> on Catherine Street off the main market square; and during the summer months in the proprietor's outdoor coffee garden just outside the town walls, near the East Gate. The concerts, all free of charge, ended with Gottfried Zimmermann's death in 1741. Apart from showcasing his earlier orchestral repertoire such as the <a href="/wiki/Brandenburg_Concertos" title="Brandenburg Concertos">Brandenburg Concertos</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orchestral_Suites_(Bach)" title="Orchestral Suites (Bach)">Orchestral Suites</a>, many of Bach's newly composed or reworked pieces were performed for these venues, including parts of his <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Clavier-%C3%9Cbung" title="Clavier-Übung">Clavier-Übung</a></i></span> (<i>Keyboard Practice</i>), his violin and <a href="/wiki/Keyboard_concertos_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Keyboard concertos by Johann Sebastian Bach">keyboard concertos</a> and of course the eponymous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coffee_Cantata" title="Coffee Cantata">Coffee Cantata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stauffer_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stauffer-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Middle_years_of_the_Leipzig_period_.281730.E2.80.931739.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_years_of_the_Leipzig_period_(1730–1739)">Middle years of the Leipzig period (1730–1739)</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bach_Seal.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1338" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Bach_Seal.svg/260px-Bach_Seal.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Bach_Seal.svg/390px-Bach_Seal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Bach_Seal.svg/520px-Bach_Seal.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bach_Seal.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bach's seal (centre), used throughout his Leipzig years. It contains the superimposed letters <i><span class="nowrap">J S B</span></i> in mirror image topped with a crown. The flanking letters illustrate the arrangement on the seal.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1733, Bach composed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_for_the_Dresden_court_(Bach)" title="Mass for the Dresden court (Bach)">Kyrie–Gloria Mass in B minor</a> which he later incorporated in his Mass in B minor. He presented the manuscript to the Elector in an eventually successful bid to persuade the prince to give him the title of Court Composer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baroquemusic_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-baroquemusic-72">[69]</a></sup> He later extended this work into a full mass by adding a <span title="Latin-language text"><span lang="la">Credo</span></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><span lang="la">Sanctus</span></span>, and <span title="Latin-language text"><span lang="la">Agnus Dei</span></span>, the music for which was partly based on his own cantatas and partly original. Bach's appointment as Court Composer was an element of his long-term struggle to achieve greater bargaining power with the Leipzig council. Between 1737 and 1739, Bach's former pupil <a href="/wiki/Carl_Gotthelf_Gerlach" title="Carl Gotthelf Gerlach">Carl Gotthelf Gerlach</a> held the directorship of the <i>Collegium Musicum</i>.
</p><p>In 1735 Bach started to prepare his first publication of organ music, which was printed as the <a href="/wiki/Clavier-%C3%9Cbung_III" title="Clavier-Übung III">third <i>Clavier-Übung</i></a> in 1739.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup> From around that year he started to compile and compose the set of preludes and fugues for harpsichord that would become his second book of <i>The Well-Tempered Clavier</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> He received the title of "Royal Court Composer" from <a href="/wiki/Augustus_III_of_Poland" title="Augustus III of Poland">Augustus III</a> in 1736.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baroquemusic_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-baroquemusic-72">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles196286–87_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles196286–87-15">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Final_years_and_death_.281740.E2.80.931750.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_and_death_(1740–1750)">Final years and death (1740–1750)</span></h4>
<p>From 1740 to 1748 Bach copied, transcribed, expanded or programmed music in an older <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphonic</a> style (<i><a href="/wiki/Stile_antico" title="Stile antico">stile antico</a></i>) by, among others, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pierluigi_da_Palestrina" title="Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina">Palestrina</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BNB_I/P/2" title="BNB I/P/2">BNB I/P/2</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johann_Caspar_Kerll" title="Johann Caspar Kerll">Kerll</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_241" title="BWV 241">BWV 241</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Torri" title="Pietro Torri">Torri</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_Anh._30" title="BWV Anh. 30">BWV Anh. 30</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Bassani" title="Giovanni Battista Bassani">Bassani</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_1081" title="BWV 1081">BWV 1081</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Gasparini" title="Francesco Gasparini">Gasparini</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missa_Canonica_(Gasparini)" title="Missa Canonica (Gasparini)">Missa Canonica</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Caldara" title="Antonio Caldara">Caldara</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_1082" title="BWV 1082">BWV 1082</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup> Bach's own style shifted in the last decade of his life, showing an increased integration of polyphonic structures and canons and other elements of the <i>stile antico</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LISA_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-LISA-81">[78]</a></sup> His fourth and last <i>Clavier-Übung</i> volume, the <i><a href="/wiki/Goldberg_Variations" title="Goldberg Variations">Goldberg Variations</a></i>, for two-manual harpsichord, contained nine canons and was published in 1741.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> Throughout this period, Bach also continued to adopt music of contemporaries such as <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">Handel</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BNB_I/K/2" title="BNB I/K/2">BNB I/K/2</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Heinrich_St%C3%B6lzel" title="Gottfried Heinrich Stölzel">Stölzel</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_200" title="BWV 200">BWV 200</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> and gave many of his own earlier compositions, such as the <i>St Matthew</i> and <i>St John</i> Passions and the <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Eighteen_Chorale_Preludes" title="Great Eighteen Chorale Preludes">Great Eighteen Chorale Preludes</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup> their final revisions. He also programmed and adapted music by composers of a younger generation, including <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Pergolesi" title="Giovanni Battista Pergolesi">Pergolesi</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_1083" title="BWV 1083">BWV 1083</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> and his own students such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlieb_Goldberg" title="Johann Gottlieb Goldberg">Goldberg</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BNB_I/G/2" title="BNB I/G/2">BNB I/G/2</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1746 Bach was preparing to enter <a href="/wiki/Lorenz_Christoph_Mizler" title="Lorenz Christoph Mizler">Lorenz Christoph Mizler</a>'s <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Correspondierende_Societ%C3%A4t_der_musicalischen_Wissenschaften&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Correspondierende Societät der musicalischen Wissenschaften (page does not exist)">Society of Musical Sciences</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Correspondierende_Societ%C3%A4t_der_musicalischen_Wissenschaften" title="de:Correspondierende Societät der musicalischen Wissenschaften">de</a>]</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup> In order to be admitted Bach had to submit a composition, for which he chose his <a href="/wiki/Canonic_Variations_on_%22Vom_Himmel_hoch_da_komm%27_ich_her%22" title="Canonic Variations on &quot;Vom Himmel hoch da komm' ich her&quot;">Canonic Variations on "Vom Himmel hoch da komm' ich her"</a>, and a portrait, which was painted by <a href="/wiki/Elias_Gottlob_Haussmann" title="Elias Gottlob Haussmann">Elias Gottlob Haussmann</a> and featured Bach's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_1076" title="BWV 1076">Canon triplex á 6 Voc</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup> In May 1747, Bach visited the court of King <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick II of Prussia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Potsdam" title="Potsdam">Potsdam</a>. The king played a theme for Bach and challenged him to improvise a fugue based on his theme. Bach obliged, playing a three-part fugue on one of Frederick's <a href="/wiki/Fortepiano" title="Fortepiano">fortepianos</a> by <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Silbermann" title="Gottfried Silbermann">Gottfried Silbermann</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidMendelWolff1998224_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidMendelWolff1998224-90">[87]</a></sup> which was a new type of instrument at the time. Upon his return to Leipzig he composed a set of fugues and canons, and a trio sonata, based on the <i>Thema Regium</i> (theme of the king). Within a few weeks this music was published as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Musical_Offering" title="The Musical Offering">The Musical Offering</a></i> and dedicated to Frederick. The <i><a href="/wiki/Sch%C3%BCbler_Chorales" title="Schübler Chorales">Schübler Chorales</a></i>, a set of six chorale preludes transcribed from cantata movements Bach had composed some two decades earlier, were published within a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> Around the same time, the set of five canonic variations which Bach had submitted when entering Mizler's society in 1747 were also printed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nekrolog_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nekrolog-93">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two large-scale compositions occupied a central place in Bach's last years. From around 1742 he wrote and revised the various canons and fugues of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Art_of_Fugue" title="The Art of Fugue">The Art of Fugue</a></i>, which he continued to prepare for publication until shortly before his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChiapusso1968277_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChiapusso1968277-95">[92]</a></sup> After extracting a cantata, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_191" title="BWV 191">BWV 191</a> from his <a href="/wiki/Bach%27s_Missa_of_1733" title="Bach's Missa of 1733">1733 Kyrie-Gloria Mass for the Dresden court</a> in the mid-1740s, Bach expanded that <a href="/wiki/Mass_(music)" title="Mass (music)">setting</a> into his <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_B_minor" title="Mass in B minor">Mass in B minor</a> in the last years of his life. Although the complete mass was never performed during the composer's lifetime, it is considered to be among the greatest choral works in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 1749, Bach's daughter Elisabeth Juliane Friederica married his pupil <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Altnickol" title="Johann Christoph Altnickol">Johann Christoph Altnickol</a>. Bach's health was, however, declining. On 2 June, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_von_Br%C3%BChl" title="Heinrich von Brühl">Heinrich von Brühl</a> wrote to one of the Leipzig <a href="/wiki/Burgomaster" title="Burgomaster">burgomasters</a> to request that his music director, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlob_Harrer" title="Johann Gottlob Harrer">Johann Gottlob Harrer</a>, fill the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Thomaskantor</i></span> and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Director musices</i></span> posts "upon the eventual ... decease of Mr. Bach".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup> Becoming blind, Bach underwent eye surgery, in March 1750 and again in April, by the British eye surgeon <a href="/wiki/John_Taylor_(oculist)" title="John Taylor (oculist)">John Taylor</a>, a man widely understood today as a charlatan and believed to have blinded hundreds of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup> Bach died on 28 July 1750 from complications due to the unsuccessful treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidMendelWolff1998188_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidMendelWolff1998188-100">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitta1899c[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastianb03spituoftpage274mode1up_274]_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitta1899c[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastianb03spituoftpage274mode1up_274]-101">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>An inventory drawn up a few months after Bach's death shows that his estate included five <a href="/wiki/Harpsichord" title="Harpsichord">harpsichords</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Lautenwerck" title="Lautenwerck">lute-harpsichords</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violins</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">violas</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Cello" title="Cello">cellos</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Viol" title="Viol">viola da gamba</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lute" title="Lute">lute</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Spinet" title="Spinet">spinet</a>, along with 52 "sacred books", including works by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> and <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidMendelWolff1998191–197_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidMendelWolff1998191–197-102">[99]</a></sup> The composer's son Carl Philipp Emanuel saw to it that <i>The Art of Fugue</i>, although still unfinished, was published in 1751.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup> Together with one of the composer's former students, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Agricola" title="Johann Friedrich Agricola">Johann Friedrich Agricola</a>, the son also wrote the obituary ("<a href="/wiki/Bach%27s_Nekrolog" title="Bach's Nekrolog">Nekrolog</a>"), which was published in Mizler's <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Musikalische_Bibliothek&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Musikalische Bibliothek (page does not exist)"><i>Musikalische Bibliothek</i></a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Musikalische_Bibliothek" title="de:Musikalische Bibliothek">de</a>]</span>, a periodical journal produced by the Society of Musical Sciences, in 1754.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nekrolog_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nekrolog-93">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_style">Musical style</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5,13.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="481" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5%2C13.jpg/170px-Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5%2C13.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5%2C13.jpg/255px-Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5%2C13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5%2C13.jpg/340px-Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5%2C13.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5,13.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A handwritten note by Bach in his copy of the <a href="/wiki/Calov_Bible" title="Calov Bible">Calov Bible</a>. The note next to <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/2_Chronicles#5:13" title="s:Bible (King James)/2 Chronicles">2 Chronicles 5:13</a> reads: "NB Bey einer andächtigen Musiq ist allezeit Gott mit seiner Gnaden Gegenwart" (<a href="/wiki/Nota_bene" title="Nota bene"><span class="nowrap">N(ota) B(ene)</span></a> In a music of worship God is always present with his grace).</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of compositions by Johann Sebastian Bach">List of compositions by Johann Sebastian Bach</a></div>
<p>From an early age, Bach studied the works of his musical contemporaries of the Baroque period and those of prior generations, and those influences were reflected in his music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff2000166_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff2000166-104">[101]</a></sup> Like his contemporaries Handel, Telemann and Vivaldi, Bach composed concertos, suites, recitatives, <a href="/wiki/Da_capo_aria" title="Da capo aria">da capo arias</a>, and four-part choral music and employed <a href="/wiki/Basso_continuo" title="Basso continuo">basso continuo</a>. Bach's music was harmonically more innovative than his peer composers, employing surprisingly <a href="/wiki/Consonance_and_dissonance" title="Consonance and dissonance">dissonant</a> chords and progressions, often with extensive exploration of harmonic possibilities within one piece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The hundreds of sacred works Bach created are usually seen as manifesting not just his craft but also a truly devout relationship with God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerl2004123_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerl2004123-106">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup> He had taught <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_Small_Catechism" title="Luther's Small Catechism">Luther's Small Catechism</a> as the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Thomaskantor</i></span> in Leipzig, and some of his pieces represent it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeaver2007280,_289–291_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeaver2007280,_289–291-108">[105]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_chorale" title="Lutheran chorale">Lutheran chorale</a> was the basis of much of his work. In elaborating these hymns into his chorale preludes, he wrote more cogent and tightly integrated works than most, even when they were massive and lengthy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The large-scale structure of every major Bach sacred vocal work is evidence of subtle, elaborate planning to create a religiously and musically powerful expression. For example, the <i>St Matthew Passion</i>, like other works of its kind, illustrated the <a href="/wiki/Passion_of_Jesus" title="Passion of Jesus">Passion</a> with Bible text reflected in recitatives, arias, choruses, and chorales, but in crafting this work, Bach created an overall experience that has been found over the intervening centuries to be both musically thrilling and spiritually profound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bach published or carefully compiled in manuscript many collections of pieces that explored the range of artistic and technical possibilities inherent in almost every genre of his time except <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a>. For example, <i>The Well-Tempered Clavier</i> comprises two books, each of which presents a prelude and fugue in every major and minor key, displaying a dizzying variety of structural, contrapuntal and fugal techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Four-part_harmony">Four-part harmony</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1770" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg/220px-Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg/330px-Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg/440px-Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden" title="O Haupt voll Blut und Wunden">O Haupt voll Blut und Wunden</a>": the four-part chorale setting as included in the <i>St. Matthew Passion</i></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Four-part_harmony" title="Four-part harmony">Four-part harmonies</a> predate Bach, but he lived during a time when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modal_music" title="Modal music">modal music</a> in Western tradition was largely supplanted in favour of the <a href="/wiki/Tonality" title="Tonality">tonal system</a>. In this system a piece of music progresses from one <a href="/wiki/Chord_(music)" title="Chord (music)">chord</a> to the next according to certain rules, each chord being characterised by four notes. The principles of four-part harmony are found not only in Bach's four-part choral music: he also prescribes it for instance for the <a href="/wiki/Figured_bass" title="Figured bass">figured bass</a> accompaniment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitta1899c[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastianb03spituoftpage315mode1up_vol._3,_appendix_XII,_p._315]_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitta1899c[httpsarchiveorgstreamjohannsebastianb03spituoftpage315mode1up_vol._3,_appendix_XII,_p._315]-111">[108]</a></sup> The new system was at the core of Bach's style, and his compositions are to a large extent considered as laying down the rules for the evolving scheme that would dominate musical expression in the next centuries. Some examples of this characteristic of Bach's style and its influence:
</p>
<ul><li>When in the 1740s Bach staged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_1083" title="BWV 1083">his arrangement</a> of <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Pergolesi" title="Giovanni Battista Pergolesi">Pergolesi</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Stabat_Mater_(Pergolesi)" title="Stabat Mater (Pergolesi)">Stabat Mater</a></i>, he upgraded the viola part (which in the original composition plays in unison with the bass part) to fill out the harmony, thus adapting the composition to his four-part harmony style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li>When, starting in the 19th century in Russia, there was a discussion about the authenticity of four-part <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Court_chant&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Court chant (page does not exist)">court chant</a> settings compared to earlier Russian traditions, <a href="/wiki/List_of_chorale_harmonisations_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of chorale harmonisations by Johann Sebastian Bach">Bach's four-part chorale settings</a>, such as those ending his <a href="/wiki/Chorale_cantata_(Bach)" title="Chorale cantata (Bach)">Chorale cantatas</a>, were considered as foreign-influenced models. Such influence was deemed unavoidable, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup></li></ul>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096940132">.mw-parser-output .listen .side-box-text{line-height:1.1em}.mw-parser-output .listen-plain{border:none;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded{width:100%;margin:0;border-width:1px 0 0 0;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-header{padding:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded .listen-header{padding:2px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen-file-header{padding:4px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen .description{padding-top:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen .mw-tmh-player{max-width:100%}@media(max-width:719px){.mw-parser-output .listen{clear:both}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .listen:not(.listen-noimage){width:320px}.mw-parser-output .listen-left{overflow:visible;float:left}.mw-parser-output .listen-center{float:none;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-audio-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="listen-header"><a href="/wiki/Chromatic_Fantasia_and_Fugue" title="Chromatic Fantasia and Fugue">Chromatic Fantasia and Fugue</a> BWV 903 performed by <a href="/wiki/Kevin_MacLeod" title="Kevin MacLeod">Kevin MacLeod</a></div>
<div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Chromatic_Fantasia_(Bach_BWV_903).ogg" title="File:Chromatic Fantasia (Bach BWV 903).ogg">1. Fantasia</a></div>
